I feel like Boeing is just a case study of capitalism being a system that is inevitably unsustainable and reaches what can best be compared to an aircraft's stall. On an airplane, pitching the nose up is how you climb. But if you pitch it too high, the wings can't generate lift anymore and the plane starts to fall instead of fly. In capitalist business models, reducing production cost and heightening the price is how you make ever increasing profits. But at some point you can't do that without sacrificing quality and no longer justifying the price, complying with regulations, or even having the ability to produce anymore at all, and you start to bleed money and fall instead of profiting.   Boeing is also a case study of capitalism being inherently incompatible with safety, and proof that aviation should not be a private industry. Boeing's interests as a business are incompatible with the standards needed for a safe manufacturer of aircraft.
When are we going to realise capitalism is the problem and it must be stopped

having talked to an actual Boeing engineer at my job, the door issue comes down to the airline and/or manufacturer, not Boeing entirely.  They will design a plane with a dual door frame and implementation.  If airline X only wants a single, then their partner or the company that completes the plane installs a door plate to take the place of the second door panel.    So if the company that makes this change doesn't properly install the correct number and type of pins for the dummy door, failures can happen.  Said contractor also is responsible for the proper installation of the hardware for the main door, so again if it is improperly installed a failure can happen.

So at 15:50  we hear that commercial aircrafts are a commodity.  At 17:01 we hear that returning FCF to investors isn't working, and at 17:45 we get that Boeing is trying to run a Michelin restaurant with a fast-food mindset.  This is an overconstrained problem definition statement.  You can only have 2 out the following 3 (cheap, quick, quality).  Without an open acknowledgement of this reality, they are stuck in the doom-loop.  Promising all 3 to everybody means that suppliers and employees will be squeezed so hard, that the fate of GM beckons.  20 years before it declared bankruptcy,  the smart engineers and suppliers had already left the room.  As for Detroit, the smart money left the room by 1960, just like how the smart money left electronics/computers by 1980.  Mom-and-pop investors and pension funds are going to be left holding the bag.  Going public is a leading indicator of going broke.  Profitable companies stay private.  Six-sigma quality programs are trailing indicators.

I don't think the analogy to fast food vs a Michelin star restaurant is sound, because the distinction there is much more cultural than it is material. You could absolutely take the output of a particular starred restaurant and industrialize the process of its creation, but it wouldn't be the same because going to an ultra-high-end restaurant is about a lot more than just the food. At 20:20 There _is_ one crucial difference between Airbus and Boeing. Airbus is virtually a state owned company, with the countries of France, Germany, Spain and the UK having stocks in it, in addition to being the bases where parts of each Airbus aircraft is manufactured. By being virtually state owned, there is less pressure to turn in a profit, and more room for quality. Boeing on the other hand, and especially since the merger with McDonnell Douglas is entierly beholden to private investors.

Those who are close to industry and how they operate....can clearly see this as a very core and fundamental problem of internal "corporate culture". Until the "thinking" is drastically altered at the C-level suite....nothing meaningful will result. Those consultants in corporate governance reading this.... know what I mean..... you "walk-the-walk" of the "talk". This is in one way surprising that the current flawed mentality still exists at Boeing nowadays...as its the exact same 1950's era thinking that got the Detroit "Big-3" in trouble facing the Japanese discipline and focus on quality they had....all based on the teachings of an American ( Deming) who brought forth these ideas decades ago, but was at first shunned in America BUT...was embraced in Japan as a demi-God. Therein lies the difference and a definition of the "problem". If the "talk" is quality and safety and the "walk" instead inside the company is "push-production-and-get-the-units-out-the-door" under ANY circumstances.....then this corporate culture will NOT allow the lower level operational "fixes" to take hold. The operational "Fixes" can be implemented, BUT must be supported from the very-top and not just be lip service. Example....the "Toyota system"....allows any production-line worker to STOP the production line if he sees failings in the product...to correct the issues...and not only is he not afraid of C-level or "boss" repurcussions...he is applauded for doing so. In the end...this  results in better product, less "rework" and ultimately an improvement of the bottom line...as counter-intuitive as this may seem at first. Japan learned this long ago.....then much later (after near bankruptcy),  the American auto industry learned this....but apparently other large American corporations like Boeing held on to 1950's thinking and never got the message.
Boeing lost it's way because it was led that way by disasterous CEO McNerney. He came into a beautifully set up and organized company - running well, happy, highly competent technical staff, and he used old fashioned boss versus employees models - and basically wrecked the place.

I think most of the world has missed what many believe is the REAL cause of the current state of Boeing. The failure occurred because of one person - Phil Condit. He pushed for and got a merger with McDonnell Douglas (MD).  For the 5+ years before the merger, McDonnell Douglas had been in a near constant downsizing state. They were constantly struggling to compete in Military and Space ventures, and squeezing commercial airplane manufacturing. The manager-heavy company had fierce competition for management jobs, which basically trained some to be very successful at beating other managers doing basically the same jobs. In essence, MD basically inadvertently set up a management training program in survival.  In the meantime, The Boeing Company was spending millions on training every employee in the company to reduce/eliminate waste, be more efficient and effective, and to reduce as many problems in the day-to-day business as possible. Management was trained to be more open to working together regardless of specific job roles, to improve processes whenever practical.   Then the Merger occurred...   In effect, you had MD managers that had been trained for the preceding 5+ years to "survive", mixed with managers from Boeing that were trained to improve processes, reduce waste, while keeping costs manageable. It was literally like putting a bunch of sharks in a take of fish. Within a few years, 60% of the original Boeing upper management left the company. Within 10 years, 85ish% were gone! MD literally took over the company by taking over management! Boeing as you stated, was pushing to keep related groups together, so that work synergy could be done as efficiently as possible. You could walk down the hall or to another building nearby, to talk with cohorts, to solve issues. After the merger, and the MD management takeover from within, they started doing exactly the opposite. They started splitting groups and organizations that cooperated previously, into different locations, all over the United States. Cooperation became limited to online meetings and phone calls.   Had Condit kept Boeing on the path they were before the merger, Boeing could have selectively hired MD engineers and even management to deal with the increased workloads and retirement concerns that were becoming issues in Boeing, as McDonnell Douglas became smaller and smaller. There was concern that large numbers of key employees would be retiring as they reached retirement age. They could have relocated people to where Boeing has been historically located.
Aren‚Äôt they following diversity when hiring instead of safety?

We simply can't rule out pilots as a factor.  The Lion Air and Ethiopian Airlines 737 MAX accidents both were a result of poor pilot experience.  In one case, the pilots turned off MCAS as instructed, recovered the aircraft, and then proceeded to re-activate the system and get themselves and their passengers killed.    The fundamental difference is that here in the US and Europe, pilots have a very different mindset and outlook on aviation than almost anywhere else in the world.  We see a switch, we want to know what it does and how it interacts with the aircraft's systems.  Not so in other parts of the world.  A pilot being told, 'Oh, MCAS?  Don't worry about that' here in the US would then say, 'No, I do, what's it do.'  Additionally, I have seen this in my own flying career: pilots are forgetting how to fly.  We're becoming systems operators and button pushers rather than pilots.  Take a look at modern training aircraft at major flight schools like ATP or the collegiate programs in the US.  You'll see some of the same airplanes, though the avionics are almost universally digital panels and many of the aircraft have autopilots.  Some collegiate programs use Cirrus aircraft, which in my opinion breed complacency in pilots for a number of reasons.  None of these are good aircraft to teach basic flying skills in, and many pilots leave those programs and go work in aircraft with vastly less knowledge than is necessary to be safe or effective.  Some have no clue how to fly traditional round-dial panels and transition to those from digital aircraft avionics and suddenly have no idea what's going on when they're getting paid to learn.    An example, at my previous company, we hired several pilots out of a major flight school in the United States to ride the right seat in private jets with advanced avionics.  They were low-time pilots (sub 500 hours) and few were flight instructors.  They were almost universally poor pilots, were overly autopilot reliant, and one of them was assigned Live ATC listening hours because he couldn't talk on the radios, which he *should* have had to show he could do for his certificates.  Further, we received limited type-specific training from the company.  If we had had an emergency in one of those aircraft, and the company had had a new First Officer in the airplane, they would have had an accident.  Period.  The same is true for the accidents involving the 737 MAX and MCAS.  We as an aviation community need to do better, act like professionals, be proficient at our craft, and perform as such.

This video is the product of a large amount of aggregation and research but IMO maddeningly continued to miss the mark on tying up the problems and recognizing the fundamental concept of what is wrong with Boeing.  Fundamentally, the problem is a problem practically every hardware manufacturer has faced in the past decade and a half (since approximately 2007 at least) and has been the cause of a large number of manufacturers while others that properly executed have survived and gone on to greater profits. The more complex the manufacturing process and the product, the more difficult has been the task.  I'm talking about the earth shattering effect of Agile development which was developed in the software industry, is a major if not main reason for the success, extreme profitability and logarithmically accelerating curve of development and decreasing costs. Agile development is a seminal development in human endeavor replacing prior development methods in particular the waterfall which is characterized by milestones, specific staging and test versions and a final "golden" end product that is launched for customers. Contrary to the idea of a finished ultimate final product, Agile is a continuous cycle of incremental development steps rather than major milestones where even the customer may be involved in the testing of each incremental steps.  For every product and manufacturer, this Agile approach has to be adapted and implemented according to the requirements of that product or face the inevitability of becoming burdened by excessive and uncompetitive costs and every increasing development time as the product becomes more complex. In the case of aircraft though, there is the contradictory requirement that aircraft must be perfectly safe. The consequences of poor decisions regarding risk are so great that not every concept of Agile development can be applied because the consequences of a commercial software application running on a phone isn't the same as an aircraft crashing and this is where Boeing made some horrendous decisions and it's unknown if there are others.  Focusing specifically on MCAS, there's probably enough information in the public to piece together how and why it came about and how its implementation  was the cause of the 2 crashes.  Boeing needed to implement a competitive feature that would equal, and preferably be the competitive reason customers would choose Boeing over aircraft competitors like Airbus. The most obvious desired objective is to be more fuel efficient and performant and the decision was to implement a wing design optimized for flight cruising at a specific altitude, speed generating as much lift as possible and least amount of drag. Of course a wing optimized for those flying conditions meant that particular design and configuration wouldn't perform as well and even very poorly under other flight conditions like the slow speeds related to takeoffs and landings. Normally this issue is addressed by changing the shape of the wings by altering the size and chord (curve) of the wing by extending flaps, changing angles of flaps, doing the same with leading wing edge extensions and so on but these traditional methods involve a lot of weight, complexity and maintenance costs.  This is when Boeing had the idea to simplify wing configuration and controls by developing MCAS which applies Fly-by-Wire technology to control the aircraft flying in the unoptimized conditions of takeoffs and landings. Fly-by-Wire is not new technology and was likely introduced in the F-16 during the 1970's and is when the pilot's controls does not connect directly and mechanically to control surfaces but instead pilot inputs are sent to a computer which discerns the pilot's intent and then sends signals to the control surfaces to execute the pilot's intended action. Fly-by-Wire is by today proven and reliable technology so MCAS is a reasonable decision and I understand latest Airbus aircraft extensively implement the technology, too.  But, Boeing's implementation introduced the first ingredient to a poor final result... Somewhere in Boeing's decision making someone decided that only 2 sensory inputs determining pitch would be needed as a cost cutting move and indeed perhaps even one input is barely necessary and someone reasoned a second would provide backup but not understanding that contradictory signals from 2 contradicting inputs only results in confusion. A third input is minimally needed to break the tie or the computer is unable to make a certain proper decision.  MCAS can be engineered to perform reliably and well but if there is a problem with inputs, it can't perform and this is what caused the 2 crashes... Not only did MCAS fail but apparently the pilots likely didn't know MCAS was a brand new component of the new version of 737 they were flying so didn't know they had to turn it off and this was in addition to the basic idea of implementing MCAS as a critically needed function during the most dangerous and demanding part of any flight with least amount of time to react properly.  Today, it's been reported that the 3rd pitch sensor which had always existed as a manufacturer option at about $20000 is now installed by default and required, and pilots are fully trained and aware what they should do if an MCAS fails.  There should not be any more MCAS failures but the question remains if other latent problems exist like what likely happened to cause the door plug to blow out recently.  But don't think that Boeing is the only aircraft manufacturer that's been experiencing and addressing problems. Take a look around the Internet and you'll find some pretty serious problems experienced by Airbus, too including one that remains "undetermined and unresolved." But Airbus has been a lot luckier than Boeing in not having any major mishaps that cost lives due to faulty equipment and that may be partly due to human judgement what risks are unacceptable.  Although commercial airflight is overall extremely safe, it's in part due to the diligence, regulation, oversight and efforts of all involved. But aircraft are still highly complex machinery that is getting more complex all the time. That on one hand makes the machinery less risky and perform better but it involves continuous change which itself involves risk. But for anyone who has lived long enough, just compare the number of crashes that happen today vs even the 1970's and there's no comparison how few major accidents occur despite the enormous increase in the flying public.

I worked at AMD for eleven years from 1992-2003. Our top bean counter was CFO Rich Previte whom founder and CEO Jerry Sanders hired to do at AMD what Stonecipher attempted to do when he was CEO at Boeing. It was the only real mistake Sanders did in the entire time we knew him going back to AMD's founding in 1969.  Previte was really really good (supposedly) at managing money with Lotus 1-2-3 and, later, Microsoft Excel. But Previte know nothing about the semiconductor industry and had zero engineering background. When cash was coming in, he spent it like a drunken sailor. When cash was tight, he fired people left and right. I survived the first *TEN* layoffs during my eleven years at the company. Previte was like someone who just got their student drivers permit and barely knows who to smoothly apply the accelerator, brakes, and steering wheel. Stonecipher was the initial catalyst to overhaul the business model beginning with the design and development of the 787 Dreamliner. I believed in AMD. And I am still a fan for AMD like I am for Boeing throughout all of my commercial flight training.  Hector Ruiz took AMD off death watch and made some tough decisions after Sanders' retirement that finally got AMD back on its feet.  My former engineering boss, Dirk Meyer, eventually became CEO and that began AMD's resurgence and the current CEO Lisa Su has transformed AMD into a true market leader during her 10 years at the helm.  Boeing needs someone like Dirk Meyer or Lisa Su to turn things around. Great job reporting this story.  I worked there for 18 years where I saw the decline of Boeing from the inside.  Then, I started my second career piloting 737s and the A320 on the outside of the manufacturing realm.  The contrasts are telling, as you have pointed out.  From the king of commercial aircraft makers, to what still exists of a 100 year legacy of success, it has been both incredible and devastating to watch Boeing fall from grace in the aerospace manufacturing business.    It is not just the 737 and 787 that have been troubling.  The company that designed and built key parts of the B-2 and F-22 stealth aircraft, major parts of the Saturn 5 Apollo moon rocket and have most recently suffered problems with the CST-100 Starliner space capsule also.  These problems highlight systemic problems with program management that Boeing was once infamous as the class of the whole world for always building the best possible aerospace products.  All of which made everyone very proud to be a part of a family of employees.  By focusing first on the customers and then on providing employees everything they needed to build the best products, not only was success possible, it was inevitable. Profitability was all but guaranteed and backed up by  thousands of orders for first class products.  Boeing though, has been gravely injured by two major market changes that I would describe as failures to properly manage its business to build on its legacy of excellence in aerospace manufacturing.  The first is the response to what should have been recognized as the inevitable growth a strong competitor building clean sheet of paper airplanes.  And second, the aerospace consolidation that eventually culminated in the merger with the nearly bankrupt McDonnell/Douglas.  While arrogance caused Boeing to lose focus on what the customer wanted, corporate greed led to the mismanagement of the industry consolidation when the notion that managing a near industry monopoly resulted in surplusing way too much of the expertise both within the original company and at the acquired businesses.  The notion of ‚Äútoo big to fail‚Äù has resulted in the drive to cater to Wall Street demands for perpetual profitability instead of attention to its customers.  Accountants took over a company where design engineers once guided the business.  Cost cutting became the order of the day, in place of how best to manage the critical assets needed to build and sell the world‚Äôs best commercial, military and space products.  The original Boeing once separated the commercial aircraft business from the military business purposely because the two types of customers functioned in often diametrically opposed ways.  Cost-plus contracts are very different from civilian customer contracts where Boeing had to pay directly for delays, cost overruns and for the consequences of accidents. McDonnell/Douglas (McD), in its heyday, was a majority-military contractor beholden to the government for most of its profits.  While Boeing was the exact opposite, with its commercial business overwhelming its military business.  Most of the other companies acquired over the years were also military focused. Merging the disparate cultures of Boeing and McD has been troubling ever since the 1997 merger, with the first decade afterwards resulting in a number of notable corporate management shakeups.  I would argue that losing one of its key up and coming managers to Ford Motor Company is emblematic of the brain drain that began with the merger with McD.  Change is always difficult to deal with. But, losing the mentors of the corporate culture that guided Boeing through its ‚ÄúIf it ain‚Äôt Boeing, I‚Äôm not going‚Äù days into whatever is chosen to describe the company today has resulted in a crisis management culture, and is largely what has caused the crisis of confidence and competence that is in full display today.
They are failing the way almost all corporations are failing. Profits are put above all else. Instead of putting that money towards better pay for employees for a better product, shareholders and executives get that money. Employees are being exploited. Less work is being done at a higher price to the consumer. It's bad for everyone, inside and outside the company, unless you're the one exploiting people. It should be illegal. Corporations need some heavy financial regulation. We need to tax the rich. Too bad the rich run this country and also control its propaganda.

How Boeing Lost Its Way"  Introduction: The video explores Boeing's trajectory from a peak of success to its current challenges, with insights provided by Jon Ostrower and The Air Current's team. It begins with an earthquake in 2001 that became a catalyst for Boeing to innovate its assembly process, leading to the successful production of the 737 Next Generation (NG). However, a series of decisions and strategies, including the development of the 787 Dreamliner and the subsequent 737 MAX, have significantly impacted Boeing's standing in the aerospace industry.  Body: 1. **Success of 737 Next Generation (NG):**    - Boeing's response to the 2001 earthquake led to the successful development and production of the 737 NG.    - Lean methodology and Just in Time principles streamlined the supply chain and assembly process.    - Boeing's Renton campus became a hub for collaboration, communication, and continuous improvement, resulting in the best-selling third-generation 737.  2. **Challenges with the 787 Dreamliner:**    - Boeing's decision to develop the 787 Dreamliner with a majority composite airframe and innovative technologies.    - The company adopted a strategy reminiscent of McDonnell Douglas, relying heavily on subcontractors to design and produce parts.    - Delays, design flaws, and financial challenges plagued the 787 Dreamliner, impacting its initial promise and causing a months-long grounding in 2013.  3. **Outsourcing Strategy and Financial Implications:**    - Boeing's outsourcing strategy for the 787 Dreamliner, shifting development costs onto subcontractors.    - Enormous negotiating leverage allowed Boeing to secure advantageous contracts, but it strained relationships with suppliers.    - The financial outcome of the 787 Dreamliner fell short of expectations, showcasing the pitfalls of an overly compartmentalized development and manufacturing process.  4. **737 MAX and Return to Traditional Processes:**    - Pressured by Airbus's A320 and dealing with 787 challenges, Boeing opted for an iteration of the 737 design.    - A return to a more traditional development and manufacturing process, with a focus on working closely with selected suppliers, especially Spirit Aerosystems.  5. **Supplier Relations and "Partnering for Success" Program:**    - Boeing's "Partnering for Success" program aimed at cost reduction, demanding price cuts from suppliers.    - Suppliers faced a dilemma between accepting lower payment or losing contracts, highlighting the power imbalance in the aerospace industry.    - The program, meant to foster optimization and innovation, strained relationships and led to dissatisfaction among suppliers.  Conclusion: Boeing's journey, from overcoming challenges with the 737 NG to facing setbacks with the 787 Dreamliner and 737 MAX, reflects the complexities and risks in the aerospace industry. The shift in strategies, from collaborative innovation to a transactional approach, has impacted supplier relationships and financial outcomes. The summary encapsulates the video's main points; however, for detailed understanding, watch the entire video.

When I was growing up in Seattle, my family rode the Boeing roller coaster. Long time residents know what that is: the constant cycle of hirings and layoffs by the company. My father was always trying to get employed there, but it was always temporary as Boeing constantly expanded and contracted its workforce. So one year, we‚Äôd move into some nice new tract house in the suburbs, live well, and take advantage of all the benefits: vaccinations, regular check-ups, and lots and lots of dental appointments. The next year, Dad would get laid off, have to take jobs pumping gas, or reading water meters, or doing custodial work (or multiple combinations of the above to make ends meet), we‚Äôd move again into some shabby rathole, and no more visits to the dentist or doctor. Ironically, Boeing has (further) shot themselves in the foot regarding a new narrow-body design prospect. Even thinking about developing a new aircraft right now is a joke (just look at the ENDLESS ridiculous and embarrassing issues the "simple upgrade" 777X and KC-46 programs are dealing with!)  That being said, IF they get their entire company and system in order and working efficiently, there's no reason they couldn't build a new aircraft for a fraction of the development cost, in a fraction of the time vs. their current management structure/model.  Honestly, I'm glad they haven't pulled the trigger yet.    We're currently in a sort-of "holding pattern" - where the A32X NEO and even 737 MAX are booked out till 2030.  This is THE TIME and possibly the ONLY time that Boeing will have to actually get everything squared away internally and set the tables for a new design. Focus on producing these current aircraft (arguably proving very difficult for Boeing however) and make cash, while changing up your internal structure and having it all ready for a new generation of narrow-body aircraft when your 737 production end is in sight a few years from now.   Of course, Airbus' production of the NEO will be slowing down then as well, and they've got the advantage of essentially printing cash (they can charge whatever they want right now) till the NEO production slows in ~7yr or so. By that time, their market perception SHOULD be basically pristine and dominant - and their market share in the narrow-body arena will undoubtedly be the strongest in the industry. With that in mind AND the fact that they aren't dealing with a broken internal system, they're already primed to design and produce a winning product over anything Boeing can produce, while also bringing it to market quicker.    Ironically, Boeing's only hope is that Airbus goes the BOEING route and just re-wings the A32X family when Boeing decides to pull the trigger on their next aircraft. It won't be quite as efficient, but it'll easily beat Boeing to market by several years, have 100% commonality with the current A32X family for airlines/operators/maintenance, and should be a low-risk project for the company. Of course, Airbus could just as easily also make an entirely new aircraft (similar product rotation/position as Boeing after all) but it seems like a waste given how (relatively) young the A32X design is.   Either way, the ball is in Boeing's court, and given Airbus' position right now (nearly their entire R&D/engineering teams are free at the moment post-A380, vs. Boeing still dealing with endless MAX and 777X issues) that's an intimidating position to be in. I'd assume Airbus already has two very different scripts written up for a re-wing vs. new design depending on Boeing's move. Everyone thinks today is Boeing's darkest hour, but it could honestly get far worse if they strike out on the timing and/or innovation of their next ground-up design. It pretty much HAS to be perfect, from the first drawing, to the last delivery.

I think the Spirit issue is completely misunderstood by most.  Basically Boeing built/split a large chunk of THEIR OWN company into a separate (individually liable) company, then HUNG them out to dry, they made ZERO cash, but because they were technically separate, Boeing's profits and earnings got a huge bump (because a prime contractor was basically giving them huge components at a loss!!)   Fast forward, and Spirit has had to do everything in its power to make a single dollar, while Boeing continues to give them the middle finger (and again, I consider Spirit another building that Boeing owns essentially, that's how it was formed...) Hanging out one of your own "internal" suppliers to die while you enjoy every penny towards your annual shareholder reports is a terrible way to run a company, and it absolutely has consequences.   Honestly Boeing should have never split the division - they did it to save a buck (and had the bonus of deferring liability) but now you stuck an independent company with their own financial requirements into a corner, and THEY have to make money as well. I knew working for Spirit was a nightmare, but I didn't initially realize that it was almost entirely Boeings fault for making it so.   And now, to this day, Boeing can point fingers at "another company" which is really one of their own fabricated divisions, so they can deffer liability and blame onto someone else - instead of just getting their own $hit together and doing the right thing(s). I'm surprised there wasn't mention of one aspect which is important to the Boeing story: The lack of government oversight. If I recall, part of the reason Boeing is in the place it is is because the FAA doesn't have the resources to inspect Boeing's facilities (and depending on the elected officials in power at the time, is actively discouraged from doing so), and relies on Boeing to self-regulate itself. And of the things that go on the chopping block first when people want to save money (both public and private), it's the budget for regulators/safety/other independent oversight. Boeing, like most companies, won't fix something if, for their shareholders, it isn't broken; increased government oversight, properly funded regulators, and new legislation to give regulators better tools to crack down on companies would all help push Boeing (and other companies) to do better.

Interesting video but there's a lot missing. The main thing is that the Max wasn't exactly a Boeing only blunder. They did it under enormous pressure from the airlines that owned a lot of 737's to supply a product that could be certified as a 737 and needed 0 additional training for pilots. Of course, it was completely stupid of Boeing to do it. It was completely dumb of FAA to certify it. It's not just the problem of the engines being bigger than they fit on the small plane. It's the bigger issue of making a much more advanced plane work in the exactly same way as the previous version created decades before. And this was an almost impossible target, and they missed it by a million miles. It's quite funny how that came back to airlines who eventually shot themselves. Southwest and United are in huge trouble, since the 7 and 10 versions won't likely be certified for a long time, several years at least. American and Delta are likely in hot water too but less than these 2. Ryanair would be if the CEO wasn't as stupid as Boeing is. And the problem is not the FAA, but EASA who since the first Max blunder did the unthinkable and now does NOT accept tactically FAA certified airplanes as they did for decades (and reciprocally). And FAA would be dead if they approved the Max 7 and 10 and EASA did not. And Boeing will be dead if EASA does not certify 7 and 10, they would not survive this blunder. It's easy to figure out what happened after the Alaska door blow out. FAA is and can be under extreme pressure from politicians and Boeing, but that is worthless if the other side of the Atlantic tells them to get lost. FAA knows that. Of course, Boeing at this point is too stupid and desperate to realize anything, and I guess they'll do what can be expected of them from the last 20 years or so: another fiasco. Or they'll think they can get away with just FAA approval. My only advice? If you own Boeing stock sell it ASAP while you can still make a buck or not lose it all. They're inside a black hole, and even light can't escape from it, let alone anything else.
I think the lesson here is about the dangers of having a purely transactional outlook. Had they stuck to the core concept of making a great product they wouldn't be in this situation. Truly a lesson for the times I say.

The problem is that now, they'te trying to juggle with just one arm, and that just doesn't work. You fire people to reduce salary budgets, and you lose the talent. But then, the next one, not only you can't keep growing, you can't maintain the new rate because it was inflated by a massive, very bad decision. So, you eat into even more of your limbs to keep the money coming for just one more quarter. What happens the next? Who cares, this quarter we won.  This is the fundamental problem with capitalism. It's not enough to maintain your income, it's gotta increase, infinite growth is the game. But that's not a winnable game.

I've worked in engineering, sourcing, and manufacturing. I can tell you that when you start to incessantly financially squeeze your Tier 2 systems suppliers, you create an intensely adversarial relationship. To appear to meet costs the most experienced/expensive engineers - the ones who know everything - are laid off. Eventually the supplier becomes indifferent which leads to quality issues.
In 2017, long before a crash, NPR (KCUR) ran a story about the Max 8 sensor issue and it was not being addressed. A pilot even described a workaround and was frustrated that he couldn't inform other pilots well enough that they needed to disable the MCAS flight-control system. The day before the 2018 crash a pilot onboard that plane in the jump seat was aware of this and informed the piloting crew. The information was not passed on well enough. MCAS wasn't invented to fix the 737MAX. It was invented in the 60s and used in 707 and later 767 flying tanker variants to compensate for the change of the center of gravity position while airborne - with great success. And, of course, with appropriate pilot training. Boeing put the same system into the 737MAX, but omitted the "appropriate pilot training" bit because that was the whole point: 737 pilots not needing to go through additional training. The problem wasn't the MCAS by itself, but the FAA/Boeing plot not to tell the pilots about it.

Software engineers... PLEASE USE SANITY CHECKS!  If the AoA sensor reports level flight then in the next moment reports a 40 degree pitch that isn't real data. You really brushed over the MCAS software issue. It would have been well worth going into the story in more detail as it highlights various aspects of the aircraft manufacturing business and some of the serious mistakes Boeing made. Just a very cut down summary: 1. If an aircraft is changed too much the pilots need to be trained on the new model which costs the airlines money and that discourages them from buying that model. 2. Boeing need to make sure not too many changes were made. The accomplished this partly with the MCAS system - some software that modified the behavior of the plane to make it fly enough like the old model that training would not be required. 3. What they got wrong was: * They hid this to some extent from regulatory agencies. * They hid this to some extent from the customers and pilots in order to avoid tipping of the regulatory agencies as if either knew they might have needed extra training for the pilots. * There were some serious flaws in the new software, it was really really badly designed. * Even after the first accident they did not resolve the above and waited until the second accident.

This isn't entirely fair to Boeing and their management. A lot of the problems attributed to the issues that have plagued headlines (Poor engineering solutions, quality control system failures, lack of cash flow, push for outsourcing, and lack of talent) cannot be pinned solely on management, but the nature of the commercial aviation duopoly, and more specifically, the presence of subsidies/grants/loans/tax free savings opportunities provided to not just their major competitor, but all their competitors (Bombardier got its CSeries development costs largely from the Quebec government, Embraer gets tons of government subsidies).   Granted, these days, designing an aircraft is an expensive process, and no other company is expected to have 40 billion dollars of free capital available to design a new product, that's insanity. However,  it is those grants that have forced Boeing to cost cut in other ways and avoid clean-sheeting designs ‚Äî they just can't afford to take on such a task alone.   So no, the solution is not to change everything, the solution is simple: Boeing needs money. It does not have the luxury of taking in porkbarrel government contracts to sustain its research and development like Lockheed Martin does ‚Äî commercial aircraft are bought by private companies.

One analogy I‚Äôd use to describe the best path forward for Boeing, in my own opinion at least, is being in a deep swimming pool.   You have the scenario of someone treading water trying not to sink, but because of that they‚Äôre just wasting energy and not moving anywhere, and eventually they‚Äôll start to be in real trouble. I‚Äôve been in a spot before where I found myself having trouble getting myself moving in that pool, but I solved it by actually deliberately choosing to stop treading water, building up my strength a bit, then launching myself forward. This meant I had to let myself sink for a second or so, but once I was moving again I easily cruised over to the side of the pool.  Boeing may need to do something similar- instead of trying to keep cutting corners and ‚Äúoptimizing‚Äù until they run out of steam, their best bet may be to take a chance and burn some money. In this scenario, they go ahead and fund R&D, fund the companies they‚Äôre working with, put other projects on pause, and take a chance on making the absolute best plane they possibly can. This may put them ‚Äúunderwater‚Äù for a bit, but if they don‚Äôt dip underwater on purpose now, they‚Äôll do it inevitably later, so best to do it while they can control the plunge.

At @9:30 the the video talks about Boeing having realised it was at risk of losing market share to Airbus, who had just launched the A320neo. However, it's not so simple; Boeing had already by then lost a ton of market share to Airbus. It hid behind increased profits arising from a growing market, but by doing the 737NG they only sort of matched the A320 family.  For me, it was when they decided to do the 737NG that Boeing lost it, because it was at that point they essentially decided to - at best - share the near term market with Airbus. However, Airbus were always going to have the trump card on the next round of development; the taller legs of the European jet meant it had room to grow, and the 737 didn't. So with the NG, Boeing had also decided to hand Airbus a huge advantage when it came to the following generation. We're now seeing that play out; Boeing has the MAX 8, 9, whereas Airbus has easily got the A319neo 320neo, 321neo, 321xlr all out of one basic upgrade. What's more, passengers actually like the Airbus aircraft, and the MAX family retains the cramped ancient 1950's cabin and the too-narrow cockpit.  So that was it. They decided to share the market, and risk losing it entirely in the following generation. Short term thinking, staying in today's battle, losing the war long term.   What they should have done once they saw that Airbus 320's were selling well in early 1990s was to have built a whole new aircraft there and then. Taking a delivery holiday to get a new jet into service when your competitor is delivering only 100 or so jets per year is a lot less painful than when they're delivering 4 times that amount if not more. Better to take the hit earlier than later, especially as there would have been a good chance of preventing Airbus making off with as much market share as they did. .   Having not done that in the early 1990s, they should have done a new aircraft instead of the MAX. There are strong suggestions that Boeing actually had a new aircraft on the drawing board ready to go. Had they done that when Airbus announced the A320neo, they'd have suffered quite a lot of short term pain. But they would have come out of that with a superior aircraft and able to command high prices.   They didn't do that either.   And having not done either of those two sensible things, they were really, strongly putting themselves at severe risk of losing even more market share. Stretching the 737 yet again was always going to be hard, whereas Airbus could upgrade with ease.   These decisions of Boeings were - at best - only ever going to keep them in a position of inferiority in comparison to Airbus's line up, totally dependent on Airbus not being able to make enough aircraft to steal the whole market. Had they executed perfectly, they were always going to struggle to claw market share back (if at all).  Instead, they executed imperfectly. Dangerously so. Fatally so. Purposefully so. They also compounded this by imposing their Partnership for Poverty on suppliers. They also dropped the ball on QC, across the entire company. This has basically taken a cobbled together strategy that was anyway sub-standard, and made it a lot, lot worse.
Karl Marx predicted all of this when he described the declining rate of profit.  This is inevitable in a capitalist system, you can cut corners in the beginning to keep up profits, but you can't cut corners forever.

Consider the difference in corporate culture.  Boeing is run by bean-counters. Finance. Boeing has MBA disease. As the video explains, it's been run to maximize profit returns while cutting costs & absolving Boeing of responsibility for R&D, production & QC. It hasn't worked! Airbus, near as I can tell, is engineering-led. This makes a massive difference.   Consider computers: IBM is sales-led; HP is engineering-led. Both systems can work, but you should - as consumer - expect a different outcome. I want a consumer product developed by an engineering-led business, not one out to set sales records. And I refuse to buy from a company that is finance-led, looking for ways to cut costs & QC to maximize profits, at my expense as a user.  Cars. Toyota is sales-led: faced with the need to design EVs, they went for quick & dirty, screwed it up, and the wheels fell off. Ouch. That effort's dead. Subaru OTOH has always been engineering-led. Faced with an impossible challenge - design an EV after years of ignoring market change - they accepted partial merger with Toyota & let it build the car; Subaru would rebrand it. They noticed that THE WHEELS FELL OFF. They're pissed. They've torn up the agreement, will find a way to design their own EV or go bust doing so. Here's hoping they succeed. GM is & always has been Finance-led, and for decades has had MBA disease. They've outsourced everything they can - more each year - & haven't made money on anything they don't understand. They understand finance. So GM's profit has derived, for decades, from GM Finance, the leasing business. Cars are an inconvenience. When forced to build EVs they were going to simply outsource the whole unpleasant production thing to China. The US Inflation Reduction Act has forced a return to the US of production, but GM isn't about to do the R&D and production of EVs. They're partnering with Chinese firms, which will build the EVs in America with a GM-labeled plant. The finished product will carry a GM label but will be a Chinese EV. Expect outsourcing to work every bit as well for GM as it did for Boeing.  I buy Lenovo PCs (engineering-led culture); Subaru cars (ditto) - though our next will be a Tesla (also engineering-led). And I feel the same way about flying. I refuse to set foot in a Boeing 737 Max. Ever. I'll pay extra to fly on an airline that can assure me I'll fly on an Airbus jet. If necessary I'll change destination & travel plans.  Boeing's finance-led management valued cash returns to shareholders over passenger safety. That failed. I doubt they can undo & fix this problem: the bean counters are still in charge.  Think hard about your next purchase. Look at the company. Want to know who dominates their culture, shapes all the decisions? Look at who becomes CEO: that's the general rule. At IBM, it's always a former sales guy. At HP, an engineer. At Ford, a sales guy. At GM, finance. I know, Mary Barra has an Engineering degree - but then she got herself an MBA & hasn't looked back. She's a bean counter.

The aircraft might be a commodity but they are optimized for different markets. The A320 family is optimized for the slightly larger variants than the 737 and the A220 for the smaller variants. The 787 is bigger than the A330 and the A350 is bigger than the 787 but slightly smaller than the 777. of course there are same sized variants but optimization is usually for the middle variant.   That‚Äôs why a lot of it is also market assessment. The A380 was too big for the market. Boeing forgot the 757 replacement in the hope the 787 would make it unimportant. By the time they saw the error the Airbus A321xlr had already taken that market. So a lot of it is market assessment.   The 737 MAX was just the result of a company moving too slow and making more mistakes in the process. Boeing's decline started after CEO T. A. Wilson, an engineer, stepped down in 1986 and the board chose Frank Schrontz, an accountant, to take his place. Even my father who had worked their since 1958 agreed the company had changed for the worse.

Don't forget that Boeing's space division is having similar issues with the Starliner capsule and has lost nearly a billion dollars to the fixed-price contract they have with NASA. During the first uncrewed test mission, they almost lost the vehicle TWICE. After the second uncrewed test flight (which they self-funded because the first one went about as bad as it could go without losing the vehicle) they discovered two more issues: tape wrapped around literal miles of wiring in the capsule was flammable in certain flight conditions and needed replacing (both as a supplied part and physically in the capsules that were already built), and part of the main parachute had the potential to fail catastrophically under normal flight loads and needed to be completely redesigned.  The first crewed test flight of Starliner is currently scheduled for no earlier than April 2024, and I don't envy the two astronauts assigned to it, because I fully expect that refusing to fly on that mission will mean they never go to space.

Love this video and the whole channel. Everything Sam works on is great. However, a couple small additions to the 737 production angle.   First, part of the reason the 737 has stuck around this long is due to those training costs. When you are trained to fly an aircraft over 12,500 lbs you get ‚Äútype rated‚Äù or certified by the FAA to only fly that aircraft. Then there are rules with how often you have to go through training to make sure you are still a good pilot. Many companies have lobbied Boeing to come out with new more efficient versions of the 737 so they don‚Äôt have to have two different sets of airplanes that do the same thing, with two different pilot groups that can only fly one or the other.   Second, every 737 has to legally fly like every other 737. Now there are some variations for length, but a pilot that flew the 737-700 should be able to fly the 737-max9 with no differences training required. See the concept of a type rating above. To make the new leap engine fit they had to do drastically change the geometry of the aircraft. So much so that they essentially made a new airplane. So they had to add the MCAS system which makes the max fly like all of the other 737‚Äôs.   Sorry, very long winded, but a couple of clarifications I felt were important.

12:24 what the hell is that climb
I've been an aerospace machinist for about 8 years now. I work at a third party supplier, its a family owned company with about 100 people working there. We're AS9100 certified and make parts for commercial and military aircraft, including flight safety components. We are given a blueprint from a "middle man" supply company, sometimes given a vague description of what the component is used for, and tasked with machining it to the exact print requirements.  We can do that just fine, but having such a compartmentalized manufacturing system seems so much more prone to mistakes and misunderstanding when you have no clue what the next level of component assembly looks like. From a machinist prospective, if you want to make a precise, well designed, and quickly manufactured aircraft, something like SkunkWorks in the 50's-90's seems to make the most sense. Where you have everyone from the design team to the machinists working in close proximity in the same facility.

I don't think the fast food/Michelin restaurant analogy works. Michelin restaurants are able to produce the quality they do because they are working at small volume and are producing a pretty simple product. One person can take overall responsibility for everything they do. Boeing has to produce incredibly complex products at a very large scale (the development costs are too high for it to be affordable if you aren't producing thousands of aircraft). Working at that scale, with strict regulations that require precise consistency means being a process-driven company. There is no other way to do it. (They do need to change their mindset to have their processes focus on quality rather than cost, though - not having "double check you've put all the bolts in the door plug" in the process is completely unacceptable).

DEI is destroying what‚Äôs left of it‚Ä¶
I absolutely agree with the popular assessment that the cause of this problem was replacing the engineers with suits, but I also think that's missing the bigger picture.  This problem isn't unique to engineering.  This is a problem across all of western and western-influenced corporate culture, especially here in the US.  At some point around the mid-late 20th century, an extremely destructive idea started to gain popularity that the primary (or indeed only) purpose of any business was to make money.  Not to do whatever that company was actually in the business of doing, but to see how much they could minmax their profit margins quarter after quarter.  Suddenly it wasn't about making the best widget you could because you cared about the quality of the widgets your company was making, it was about how many corners you could cut off your widget before the drop in quality started to cost you more money than it saved you.  Corporations started to become run exclusively by MBAs willing to sacrifice everything up to and including human lives if they calculated it would put a few extra dollars in their already obscenely heavy pockets.  It's no coincidence that the rise in income inequality started around the same time people started to notice a decline in the quality of many consumer products.  The generations that remembered the gilded age and the depression had largely died off, and the younger generations that only remembered the prosperity of the post-WWII years overwhelmingly elected politicians like Reagan and Thatcher on the promise that what was good for the ultra-wealthy would ultimately benefit them (spoiler alert: it had exactly the opposite effect).  Here we are almost half a century later, and the economic situation we have today is the end result.  Greed, especially corporate greed, is a cancer that should be eradicated from this world with extreme prejudice.  Moral of the story: the ultra-rich aren't your friends, they don't care about you, and letting them get away with whatever they want is NEVER going to benefit you or anyone but them.

Here are some key takeaways from the video "How Boeing Lost Its Way":  1. Boeing's troubles began with the development of the 787 Dreamliner in 2003, when it outsourced much of the design and production to suppliers to cut costs. This led to significant delays and cost overruns.   2. In response, Boeing brought more parts of the 737 MAX development and production in-house. But in trying to rush the plane to market to compete with Airbus's A320neo, it made a flawed design that contributed to two fatal crashes.  3. Boeing has strained relationships with many suppliers by demanding lower prices, leading to defects and disruptions. Spirit Aerosystems in particular has been responsible for many issues with Boeing planes.   4. While Airbus has focused on supply chain stability and incremental innovation, Boeing has embraced a commodity mindset - trying to systematize and optimize aircraft production to increase efficiency and returns to investors. But this approach is not well suited to the complexities of aircraft manufacturing.    5. Boeing has lost a lot of talented staff and institutional know-how. It desperately needs a new aircraft but lacks the internal capabilities and finances to develop one smoothly.   6. Boeing is caught in a downward spiral of cost-cutting and quality issues that make it harder to build safe, reliable, and profitable planes. Deep organizational and cultural changes are likely needed to reverse this trend.

Maybe even since safe planes is a public good, we could all chip in to fund such an organization, so even if sales start to dry up, there is always a source of funding to ensure that planes are being built to a high standard of quality, rather than anyone being forced to try and maintain profit growth during a period like an oil crisis where of course sales are going to slow.

First, collaborating with Jon Ostrower is perfection. I had the opportunity to meet him a few times through a mutual friend when I worked supply chain for the 787.  Second, most people don‚Äôt realize there were actually two rounds of ‚ÄúPartnering for Success‚Äù and Boeing gladly went after some suppliers twice because SC Finance had some bean counters who never took into account many fixed overhead, R&D, and resource costs. If a part was made from a $100 block of aluminum and took 5 hours to machine, Boeing said the final product should be purchased at $80 because supplier could make back a lot of money on recycled metal. Nevermind manpower or insurance or amortized machine costs or downtime, etc.  Finally, the downstream impact of supplier when Boeing put the screws on Tier 1 suppliers like Spirit: the Tier 1s would do their own versions of PFS to their subtiers, squeezing more money out of them. End result of this was Spirit moving nearly 10,000 components for larger assemblies, mostly from US machine shops, in the 2017-2020 time period, to a consolidation manufacturing facility in partnership with UACE in Vietnam. Oh, then the pandemic hit and the suppliers had no ‚Äúrainy day‚Äù funds due to PFS, experienced workers ‚Äúretired‚Äù, suppliers were taking on more work for SpaceX, Blue Origin and Relativity because the cache of being a Boeing supplier was long gone.  All thanks to Phil Condit allowing McDonnell Douglas leadership, who torched that company, to take over Boeing, and let the bean counter who ran MD into the ground do the same, whole new management from GE came in to focus on using profits for stock buybacks instead of investing in future models, which Boeing had done successfully since the introduction of the 707 in the 1950s.  The name on the buildings may be Boeing, but the culture is sadly entrenched in MD ways with Jack Welch minions running the show, and the company into the ground.
